PREFACE
In the preface to Pastures New, an Account of the Pastoral
Occupation of Port Phillip, promise was made of a second publica-
tion to contain a record of all holdings under depasturing licences.
This volume is its fulfilment. The first part gives the names of the
pastoral licensees ; and in the second part (commencing on page
145), particulars of the runs which they occupied are given. A
map showing boundaries of the runs is inserted at the end of the
volume.
The story of the pastoral development of the State of Victoria
by its pioneer graziers cannot be appreciated to the full without
some account of their legal standing and the official attitude to-
wards them. In Pastures New such an account is given at some
length. Here a much condensed account is all that space will
permit. The lists following contain no holding that was not in
existence during the sixteen years extending from 1834 to 1851,
from the earliest settlement to the proclamation of the separate
colony of Victoria.
Prior to 1855, the lands of Australia were held to be the property
of the Crown, as represented by His Majesty’s Government in
London. Occupation of them, save by purchase or free grant, was
not authorised until 1833, when Acts of the Legislative Council in
Sydney placed the “ squatters” under the control of Commissioners
of Crown Lands, known by the abbreviated designation of C.C.L.
Further Acts in 1836 and 1838 augmented the authority of these
officials and tightened up the conditions. An annual fee of £lO for
each licensed run was imposed, the extent of the run being im-
material. The imposition of this fee was at first fiercely opposed,


and where at all possible evaded. This was the period of the in-
vasion of Port Phillip by the over-straiters from Van Diemen’s
Land and the overlanders from the Sydney side and Monaro.
The pastoral laws were further amended in March 1839. A force,
known as the Border Police, was formed to act as escort to the C.C.L.
who, of course, was also a magistrate. They did very useful work,
besides imparting dignity to the Commissioner’s position. The
cost of this new force was met by the imposition of an annual stock
assessment of one half-penny per sheep, three half-pence for cattle
and three pence for horses. In practice, stocking up so that an
assessment might be levied, became an essential condition for the
holding of a depasturing licence. This assessment was removed in
1853 for a time : but was re-enacted at enhanced rates. In 1844,
Governor Gipps decreed without consulting his newly enlarged
Council, that the £lO fee was to be limited to an area with a
maximum of 12,800 acres of a grazing capacity of 4000 sheep or
640 cattle : for each area of a capacity of 1000 sheep, a further
charge of £2 10s. was made. Passive resistance led to suspension
for a period. Smarting under the continued prickings of official-
dom, the Pastoralists’ Association came into being, and the venue
of the conflict transferred to the old country. Here success was
almost immediate.
On the 29th August, 1846, the Imperial Parliament passed an
Act under which at Osborne House, Isle of Wight, Queen Victoria, in
March 1847, signed Orders-in-Council wherein were recognised, for
the first time, the rights of the pastoralists, and bestowed upon
them a definite standing in law. They formed the Magna Charta
of the Port Phillip Pastoralists, though from official inertia, the gold
discoveries and separation from the Mother Colony, effect was
never fully given them. The squatter thenceforward was not under
the autocratic thumb of the C.C.L., and no longer was a mere
sojourner on the land. He now possessed a right, outside of certain
limited areas known as the Settled Districts, to a lease for fourteen
years of the lands in his occupation at the time of the Australian
publication of the Orders, 7th October, 1847. Rentals remained as
before. During the currency of the lease, no person other than
the lessee could purchase any portion ; the lessee could, after
formal application, buy at not less than £1 per acre in lots of 160


acres, the sites of his improvements ; and at its expiry, the whole or
any part. In the Intermediate Districts, which comprised any
lands in the Counties of Bourke, Grant and Normanby, and the
Gippsland District, not included in the Settled Districts, similar
conditions ruled, but the term was eight years. In the settled
districts, that is all the lands within three miles of the coast line
excluding the shore lines of the Port Phillip and Western Port Bays ;
within twenty-five miles of Melbourne ; fifteen of Geelong ; ten of
Alberton and Portland ; and two miles on each side of the Glenelg
River, from its mouth to the confluence of the Crawford River, the
leases were for twelve months only, without any right of pre-emptive
purchase. In all three instances the Crown had the right of re-
entry for a great number of public purposes.
On publication of the Orders, all persons in occupation of licensed
runs had to lodge applications for leases, stating the name, district,
area, carrying capacity, boundaries, and period of occupation.
Naturally, the boundaries claimed frequently overlapped those of
adjacent runs. The applications were sorted out into districts,
those for the Wimmera being published in the Port Phillip Govern-
ment Gazette of 19th July, 1848 ; Murray District, 26th July ;
Gippsland, 23rd August; Western Port, 4th October ; supplement-
ary lists in December ; while the large district of Portland Bay did
not appear until 23rd February, 1849. The Counties of Bourke and
Grant completed the series, 14th March. Lists of caveats were
advertised in April 1849. Commissioners of Disputed Boundaries
were appointed, and were busily engaged for several years. The
number of each run applied for under the Orders is given together
with its acreage and estimated carrying capacity.
The location of the first licences issued, 1838, was given simply
as Port Phillip. In 1840, Melbourne and Geelong were the localities
named. A few licences were issued by the Commissioners at Tumut
(Morumbidgee) and Cooma (Monaro) for runs within what was to
be the Port Phillip District. Portland Bay, containing but two
run-holders, did not yet officially exist. In July 1840, Port Phillip
was divided into two parts ; No. 9 or Western Port District, in-
cluding all Port Phillip up to the Main or Bayangan Range, between
the Ovens and Goulburn Rivers ; and No. 10 or Portland Bay
District, embracing all the country west of the Werribee River and a


line north from it to the Murray River. On the 13th September,
1843, No. 9 District was cut into three ; the Gippsland District,
all the country south of the Dividing Range and east of a line from
Anderson’s Inlet north to the Alps (nearly as we know it to-day) ;
the Murray District, between the Alps, the Murray and Goulburn
Rivers ; and the Western Port District the balance. Three counties
or reputed counties, Bourke, Grant and Normanby, were proclaimed
as separate Districts, though the last was practically ignored. No.
10 District was divided by a line along the Dividing Range to the
South Australian Boundary into Portland Bay and Wimmera
Districts, 9th November, 1846.
Spelling of run names was somewhat erratic. The official
spelling, though often hopelessly wrong, has generally been adopted.
It remains to be added that the publication of these records and
the preceding volume, Pastures New , has been rendered possible by
the generosity of a number of descendants of the pioneers of Port
Phillip.


FOREWORD
In this work the names of the true pioneers of Port Phillip, and
of the properties they held under depasturing licenses, are per-
petuated. With them is associated perhaps the most successful
colonisation achievement the world has seen. Hitherto no adequate
record of the pastoral occupation of the Colony, which came to be
known as Victoria, had been compiled, but by the task now com-
pleted material of exceptional value is added to our historic data.
Frequently we read of the gold fields days as the principal
period of Australian development. It is well to reflect, however,
that long before gold mining became a factor in the affairs of this
country, nearly all the land south of the Murray fit for grazing had
been explored and occupied. Those colonists who explored and
occupied the country laid as well the foundations of national
stability* \iithout assistance or encouragement, often in the face of
opposing forces, they subdued the wilderness. They established
flocks and herds of unrivalled excellence, and from the primeval
bush they fashioned some of the most noted properties of the
Empire.
The march of progress and subdivision have long since obliterated
the old pastoral runs, and few of the famous land marks of the early
colonial days still remain, but the fascinating story of the pastoral
settlement of Port Phillip will live to interest and inspire future
generations of British people. That story has been well told in
Pastures New , the forerunner of this publication.


It is a pleasure to contribute a foreword to this work, in which
1 am deeply interested, and I would venture the prediction that in
times to come many people scattered throughout the world, de-
scended from our pioneers, will point with pride to their forebears
among the sturdy Port Phillip pastoralists, whose names are en-
rolled in the records of these pages.
H. M. STRACHAN.

ARMYTAGE, Thos. ; h. 1820 ; took up with Chas. Franks, who was
killed by the blacks, Mt. Cotterell, for his father in 1836. He
then took up Ingleby, where d. in 1842. He was articled as
clerk to J. T. Gellibrand, of Port Phillip Association. All
Armytage holdings were in firm name of Armytage and Sons.

DERWENT Co.; Capt. Swanston, Major Wm. Mercer, Geo.
D. Mercer, and Thos. Learmonth, David Fisher, mgr.
(The Derwent Co. was composed of the remnants of the Port
Phillip Assn, which sent John Batman over the Strait, but
failed to secure any valuable concession from the British
Government. They bought out the rest and purchased 9416
acres close to Geelong, £7OOO being allowed to cover the out-
of-pocket expenses of the assn. It was dissolved in 1842,
the properties being divided up by mutual agreement) :
